God's Power Before Creation
Maturidi answers a question about God's pre-creative power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's power is eternal, essential, and unlimited, as demonstrated by the existence of created things.

Core Idea
God's power is not limited to what He has created; it is essential and infinite.

This passage addresses a question about God's power before creation. Maturidi explains that God was always able to create, because incapacity is impossible for Him. The very existence of the universe is a sign of His eternal power, which is an essential attribute, not an acquired one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Let the words of the author sink in before diving into analysis.
Notice the question-and-answer format.
2
Identify the proof structure
The passage contains a question, an answer, and evidence. Identify each part and see how the evidence supports the answer.
The evidence has two parts: impossibility of incapacity and the existence of originated things.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider what it means that God's power is essential and not accidental. How does this affect the scope of His power?
This leads to the conclusion that His power covers all possibilities without number.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تم سئل عن الله : أكان قادرا على أنه خلق الأشياء قبل خلقها ؟ فزعم نعم ، دليله أن العاجز ممنوع ، فدل وجود المحدث على قدرته ، وإذا كان هو قادرا بذاته . لا بما يعرض من القدرة ، فهو موصوف بالقدرة على الدنيا وأمثالها مما لا يحصى .
Then he was asked about God: Was He able to create things before their creation? He claimed yes, his evidence is that the incapable [is] prevented, so the existence of the originated indicates His power. And if He is powerful by His essence, not by an accident of power, then He is described with power over the world and its like without number.
Source pages p. 732

Commentary
This passage is a question-and-answer proof. The interlocutor asks whether God was able to create things before He actually created them. The answer is affirmative. The proof is that if God were not able, He would be incapable, and incapacity is impossible for God. The existence of contingent things proves His power, because they are brought into existence. Furthermore, God's power is essential to Him, not an accidental attribute that comes and goes. Therefore, His power is eternal and unlimited, extending over all possible things—the world and everything like it without restriction.

Key Terms
قادر
قدرة
ذات
عاجز
محدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's power is limited to what He actually created, as if He could not have created more or less.
Correction: The passage teaches that God's power is essential and infinite, covering all possible things without number, not just what He chose to create.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the evidence that God is powerful?
A) The existence of contingent things
B) The fact that He is not incapable
C) Both A and B
D) The question itself

Key Takeaway
God's power is essential, eternal, and encompasses all possibilities, as proven by the existence of the universe and the impossibility of His incapacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Since God's power is essential and infinite, not limited to what He has created, what situation in your life have you assumed is beyond His ability to change? Why do you hesitate to bring it before Him in full trust? Today, write it down and pray with the conviction that His power encompasses all possibilities.
?
The proof says the very existence of created things indicates God's power. When you face a trial or a blessing, do you habitually trace it back to God's power, or do you attribute it to natural causes or luck? What would change in your heart if you consciously saw every event as a direct sign of His qudra?
?
God is powerful by His essence, not by an acquired power. Does your reliance on God reflect that His power is inherent and eternal, or do you secretly doubt that He can handle the 'new' problems of modern life? What steps can you take to deepen your tawakkul, knowing His power never runs out?

Why Asking 'Why Did God Create?' Is Misplaced
Maturidi argues that the question about creation's purpose is invalid and redirects to worship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that asking about the cause of God's actions is impossible and how this redirects our focus to worship and obedience.

Core Idea
Asking 'why' about God's actions is like questioning the sovereign—impossible and misplaced.

This passage presents Maturidi's response to those who ask why God created when He does not benefit. Maturidi reframes the question entirely: rather than seeking a cause for God's actions, we should focus on recognizing His lordship and fulfilling our duties. He declares that asking about the 'cause' is logically impossible because no one has authority over God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the question
The passage begins with a question about God's purpose in creation. Note the assumption that God must have a reason or benefit.
2
Understand the faulty answer
The answer given (that God created for reward) is shown to be off-topic. Recognize why Maturidi dismisses it.
3
Grasp the key argument: impossibility of asking about cause
Maturidi states that asking about the cause (al-`illa) is impossible because no one has authority over God. Consider what this means for theological questions.
4
Redirect focus to worship and obedience
The proper response is to occupy ourselves with recognizing God's lordship, obeying Him, and preparing answers for what He teaches—not seeking reasons for His actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم سئل عن خلقه الأشياء إذ لم يكن له فيه نفع ولا كان عابئا به، فزعم أنه خلق العرض على ثواب الأبد. وذلك حكمة، فيكون فعله لنفع يكون لخلقه لا لعلمة تقدمت الخلق، وهو كاتحاد البنيان وأنواع الأشياء يحدث من قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : وقد بينا نحن ما يقتضى هذا الحرف من الجواب عن الحكمة فنسأل عنه العباد على أن السؤال عن العلة محال ؛ لإحالة أنه يكون لأحد عليه سلطان ، أو يخرج فعله وبعد، فإن السؤال عن تعرف حكمة الربوبية وحق ربوبيته علينا معرفته ومعرفة حقه وأمره والقيام بما علينا من طاعته وتعظيمه ، والإعداد لحق الجواب في كل ما يقوله ويعلمه ، وذلك يُشغلنا عن طلب الاعتلال له في فعله ، أو الاحتجاج بالجواب عنه فما تعدى السائل طوره ، وأعرض عما عليه من أعذاره لفعله الذي هو مسئول عنه مجزي به، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وقوله خلق الخلق لنفع الخلق ونفعه، ما ذكر فانه حيد عن الجواب ؛ لأنه سئل عن خلق الأشياء ، ومن ذكر فهم صنف من الجملة ؛ فلذلك أوجب ذلك حيده وعلى ذلك شأن القدرية فيما يسألون عن خلق الأفعال فيرجعون في الجواب . إلى فعل الكفر والمعاصى ، وذلك فاسد ؛ لأن طريق هذا سمعي ، والأول الذي وصف عقلي قال الشيخ رحمه الله : والأصل عندنا أن الله تعالى لم يخلق خلقا إلا وأثر نعمه عليه ظاهر ، وأدلة جوده فيه بين ، وأنه حكمته بما فيه من دلالة وحدانية موجدها و برهان سلطانه ونفاذ مشيئة فيه محقق ، وعلامة قدرته وعلمه بحقائق الأشياء غير خفى في ذلك الم ولم كانت الحكمة ؟ والسؤال على أنك لم أنعمت ؟ أو لماذا أظهرت جودك ؟ أنت حجة وحدانيتك ؟ إلى آخر ما ذكر محال فاسد لا يقبله عقل ولا يحتمله وسع لقيحه ؛ لذلك بطل هذا النوع من السؤال ، وبالله التوفيق .
Then he was asked about His creation of things when there was no benefit to Him in it, nor was He occupied with it. He claimed that He created the accident for eternal reward. And that is wisdom, so His action is for a benefit to His creation, not for a knowledge that preceded creation. It is like the cohesion of a building and the types of things that occur. Abu Mansur said, may God have mercy on him: And we have already shown what this expression requires of an answer about wisdom, so we ask about it from the servants. Indeed, asking about the cause is impossible, because it is impossible that anyone has authority over Him, or that His action is outside [His will] and after [the fact]. So the question is about recognizing the wisdom of lordship and the right of His lordship upon us: to know Him and His right and His command, and to fulfill what is upon us of obedience to Him and glorification, and preparation for the correct answer in everything He says and teaches. And that occupies us from seeking a reason for His action or arguing with an answer about it. So the questioner has transgressed his bounds and turned away from what is upon him of excuses for his own action for which he will be asked and requited. And there is no power except with God. And His statement that He created creation for the benefit of creation and His benefit—what he mentioned is a deviation from the answer, because he was asked about creating things, and mentioning some is a type from the whole; therefore that necessitated his deviation. And upon that is the state of the Qadariyya when they are asked about the creation of actions and they return in the answer to the act of disbelief and sins. And that is invalid because the way of this is scriptural, while the first which was described is rational. The Shaykh said, may God have mercy on him: And the principle with us is that God the Exalted did not create any creation except that the effect of His blessings upon it is apparent, and the proofs of His generosity in it are clear, and that His wisdom is by what is in it of indication of the oneness of its originator and the proof of His authority and the execution of His will is realized, and the sign of His power and knowledge of the realities of things is not hidden in that. Did you not ask: what is the wisdom? And the question: 'Why did you not bestow favor?' or 'Why did you show Your generosity?' or 'You are the proof of Your oneness?' to the rest of what is mentioned is impossible, invalid, which no intellect accepts and whose narrowing does not allow? Therefore this type of question is invalid. And by God is success.
Source pages p. 733

Commentary
This passage is a set of questions and answers about the purpose of creation, followed by a statement of the Maturidi principle. The questioner asks: why did God create things when He does not benefit and was not concerned? The answer given (attributed to the opponent) is that God created for reward, and that is wisdom—acting for the benefit of creatures. But the author notes that this answer is off-topic because it focuses on a subset. Then the author (Abu Mansur) says that the proper response is that asking about the 'cause' (al-`illa) of God's actions is impossible because no one has authority over Him. Instead, humans should focus on recognizing His lordship, obeying Him, and preparing correct answers. Asking for a purpose behind God's actions diverts from this duty. The author also criticizes the Qadariyya for similar errors. Finally, the author states the principle: everything God creates shows His blessings, generosity, and proofs of His unity, power, and knowledge. Therefore, questions like 'why did You not bestow favor?' or 'why did You show generosity?' are invalid—they are incompatible with sound reason.

Key Terms
حكمة
خلق
علة
الربوبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God must have a reason for creation similar to human motives.
Correction: Asking for the 'cause' of God's actions is invalid because He is sovereign and no one can question His authority.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is it invalid to ask about the cause (al-`illa) of God's actions?
A) Because God does not act for a reason
B) Because no one has authority over God
C) Because the answer is known from revelation
D) Because God has explained it already

Key Takeaway
Asking about the purpose of God's actions is misplaced; our duty is to recognize His lordship and obey.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever doubted God's wisdom in your life circumstances, demanding to know 'why' before you obey? What would it look like to instead prepare for the correct answer as this lesson commands?
?
The passage says the questioner ‘transgressed his bounds.’ In what specific area of your life are you most tempted to cross from being a servant to being a judge of God’s actions? What one step can you take today to return to your proper role?
?
Imagine standing before God and being asked about your own actions, not His. How does this shift your focus from seeking reasons for God’s deeds to seeking excuses for your own? What excuse are you relying on most right now?

The Principle: Creation Always Shows Divine Blessings and Proofs
Maturidi states the principle that all creation displays God's blessings and proofs, making certain questions invalid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi principle that every creation manifests God's blessings and serves as evidence of His unity, power, and knowledge.

Core Idea
Everything God creates is a sign of His generosity and a proof of His oneness.

In this second part, Maturidi refutes another faulty answer and criticizes the Qadariyya for similar errors. He then lays down the principle that everything God creates displays His blessings and proofs of His unity, power, and knowledge. This makes any question like 'why didn't you bestow favor?' logically impossible.

Guided Path
1
Examine the faulty answer and its critique
Maturidi shows that saying 'God created for benefit' is too narrow; it only addresses part of creation.
2
Critique of the Qadariyya
He compares this error to the Qadariyya's way of answering questions about actions.
3
Learn the fundamental principle
The principle: every creation has apparent blessings and proofs of God's generosity and oneness.
4
Recognize the invalidity of certain questions
Questions like 'why did You not bestow favor?' are impossible because the proofs are already evident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم سئل عن خلقه الأشياء إذ لم يكن له فيه نفع ولا كان عابئا به، فزعم أنه خلق العرض على ثواب الأبد. وذلك حكمة، فيكون فعله لنفع يكون لخلقه لا لعلمة تقدمت الخلق، وهو كاتحاد البنيان وأنواع الأشياء يحدث من قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : وقد بينا نحن ما يقتضى هذا الحرف من الجواب عن الحكمة فنسأل عنه العباد على أن السؤال عن العلة محال ؛ لإحالة أنه يكون لأحد عليه سلطان ، أو يخرج فعله وبعد، فإن السؤال عن تعرف حكمة الربوبية وحق ربوبيته علينا معرفته ومعرفة حقه وأمره والقيام بما علينا من طاعته وتعظيمه ، والإعداد لحق الجواب في كل ما يقوله ويعلمه ، وذلك يُشغلنا عن طلب الاعتلال له في فعله ، أو الاحتجاج بالجواب عنه فما تعدى السائل طوره ، وأعرض عما عليه من أعذاره لفعله الذي هو مسئول عنه مجزي به، ولا قوة إلا بالله . وقوله خلق الخلق لنفع الخلق ونفعه، ما ذكر فانه حيد عن الجواب ؛ لأنه سئل عن خلق الأشياء ، ومن ذكر فهم صنف من الجملة ؛ فلذلك أوجب ذلك حيده وعلى ذلك شأن القدرية فيما يسألون عن خلق الأفعال فيرجعون في الجواب . إلى فعل الكفر والمعاصى ، وذلك فاسد ؛ لأن طريق هذا سمعي ، والأول الذي وصف عقلي قال الشيخ رحمه الله : والأصل عندنا أن الله تعالى لم يخلق خلقا إلا وأثر نعمه عليه ظاهر ، وأدلة جوده فيه بين ، وأنه حكمته بما فيه من دلالة وحدانية موجدها و برهان سلطانه ونفاذ مشيئة فيه محقق ، وعلامة قدرته وعلمه بحقائق الأشياء غير خفى في ذلك الم ولم كانت الحكمة ؟ والسؤال على أنك لم أنعمت ؟ أو لماذا أظهرت جودك ؟ أنت حجة وحدانيتك ؟ إلى آخر ما ذكر محال فاسد لا يقبله عقل ولا يحتمله وسع لقيحه ؛ لذلك بطل هذا النوع من السؤال ، وبالله التوفيق .
Then he was asked about His creation of things when there was no benefit to Him in it, nor was He occupied with it. He claimed that He created the accident for eternal reward. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you ever catch yourself complaining about what you lack, forgetting that every single thing around you is a sign of God's generosity? What would it take for you to truly see the blessing in a difficult situation you are facing right now? Commit to one concrete way you will remind yourself of this principle today.
?
When you see the vastness of creation, from the stars to your own body, do you genuinely see proofs of God's oneness and power, or do you take them for granted? What step can you take this week to deepen your awareness of these signs?
?
The passage says it is impossible for an intellect to question God's favor. Yet, do you ever find yourself internally asking, 'Why didn't I get that?' or 'Why this trial?' How can you train your heart to surrender such questions, trusting that all creation is already a manifestation of His wisdom and generosity?

Ibn Shabib's Main Argument for the Temporality of Bodies
Ibn Shabib argues bodies are temporal because they are never free from temporal motion or rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Shabib's core argument that bodies are temporal because they are never free from temporal motion or rest.

Core Idea
Bodies are temporal because they cannot precede the temporal attributes they always bear.

Muhammad ibn Shabib presents a concise proof for the temporality of bodies. Before exploring objections, it is essential to grasp his starting point: a body is never found without either rest or motion. Since both are temporal and the body cannot precede them, the body itself must be temporal. This lesson walks through that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the technical terms: سكون, حركة, مقام, ظعن.
2
Identify the core premise
Ibn Shabib begins with the claim that a body is never free from rest (sukūn) or motion (ḥaraka). Identify this statement and notice how he defines rest as 'staying' (muqām) and motion as 'departure' (ẓa‘n).
3
Understand how motion is known through sense
The passage explains that motion is known through the necessity of observing a thing's change of place. Even though motion itself is not directly sensed, we know it by the difference of state in the sensible world.
This is the argument from 'otherness' (ghayriyya) — motion is an accident distinct from the body.
4
Follow the conclusion
Since the body does not precede the temporal accidents (motion/rest) that always inhere in it, the body itself must be temporal. Connect this back to the premise.
The analogy with Zayd and Amr illustrates that motion belongs to the body regardless of other bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج محمد بن شبيب فى حدث الأجسام بما لا يخلو من سكون هو مقام ، وحركة هي الظعن ، وهما محدثان بما يختلف على الإثنين المكان بما تقدم أحدهما ، ثبت أن قد حدث فى أحدهما ، ولو سمى ذلك مادة في أحد الجسمين فالقول فيها أيها حدثت ، وفى حدوث غير ما قلنا يعلم بحدوث الزوال وجود من الجسم في غير موضع تراه في الأول ، وبهذه الضرورة التي أظهرت في الجسم الانتقال علمنا الحركة التي لا تحس ؛ إذ وجدنا اختلاف الحال في المحسوس وعرفنا باعتماد الشىء فى المكان الأول وانتقاله فى المكان الثاني أن المسمى اعتماده في الحال الأول يصير حركة ونقلة فى الحالة الثانية مما لا يوصف الحركة بمناسبة الجسم ولا مباينته ؛ إذ ذلك حق الجسم ، ثم زيد أحق من عمرو بها لأن توهم عدم عمرو لا ينعدمها عن زيد إذا وجد هو فى غير المكان الأول . وأجاب المعارض له أن كيف هي ؟ إذ هي فعلكم ، ولم يعرف كيفية فعله قبله ، فلم استدللتم على الحركة بالمقاييس ؟ قيل : إنما يعرف أن كيف التقدم والتأخر الذى هو فعلنا ، لا أن يُعرف غيريتها لنا ، وإنما أقمنا الدلالة على الغيرية . ألا ترى أن قوما أنكروا الغيرية للجسم على إثبات القول بالتقدم والتأخر ، ثم إذ ثبت حدث ما ذكر - والجسم لا يسبقه . ثبت حدثه
And Muhammad ibn Shabib argued for the temporality of bodies by (saying): A body is never free from rest, which is staying, and motion, which is departure. Both are temporal due to what differs regarding the two: the place by what preceded one of them. It is established that one of them has occurred. And if that is called a material in one of the two bodies, then the statement about it is whichever occurred. And in the occurrence of other than what we said, it is known by the occurrence of departure and existence of the body in a place you do not see in the first. And by this necessity which manifested in the body transition, we know motion which is not sensed; for we found difference of state in the sensible and we knew by the thing's resting in the first place and its transition to the second place that what is called its resting in the first state becomes motion and transition in the second state. Motion is not described by commensurability with the body nor by its distinctness; for that is the right of the body. Then Zayd is more deserving of it than Amr because the assumption of the non-existence of Amr does not entail its non-existence for Zayd when he exists in other than the first place. And the objector answered him: How is it? For it is your action, and you did not know the modality of its action before it. So why do you infer motion by analogies? It was said: It is only known that the modality of precedence and posteriority is our action, not that its otherness for us is known. We only established the evidence for otherness. Do you not see that some people denied otherness for the body while affirming the statement of precedence and posteriority? Then when the occurrence of what was mentioned is established — and the body does not precede it — its temporality is established.
Source pages p. 736

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Shabib's proof for the temporality of bodies (huduth al-ajsam). The argument begins with the premise that a body is never devoid of rest (sukun) or motion (haraka), both of which are temporal. Since the body cannot precede them, it must be temporal. The passage then elaborates on how motion is known through sensory observation of change of place, even when motion itself is not directly sensed. It addresses an objection that motion is only known by analogy from human actions. The response clarifies that we know motion through its effects and otherness (ghayriyya) from the body. The conclusion is that the body is temporal because it does not precede the temporal attributes that inhere in it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think motion is a property of the body itself.
Correction: The argument distinguishes motion as a separate accident (otherness) that inheres in the body, proving the body's temporality.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Shabib, why are rest and motion considered temporal?
A) Because they are created by Allah
B) Because they differ with respect to place and one precedes the other
C) Because they are sensed directly
D) Because they are eternal

Key Takeaway
Ibn Shabib argues that bodies are temporal because they are never free from temporal motion or rest, and the body cannot precede them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your body is never free from temporal motion or rest, what does that constant change say about your utter dependence on the Creator every moment? Do you live with that awareness or do you feel self-sufficient?
?
Ibn Shabib argues that motion is known through change of place. When you move from one state to another—from health to sickness, from joy to sorrow—do you see the hand of Allah creating each new state, or do you attribute change to mere chance?
?
Motion is an accident distinct from your body, not a property of yourself. How does this truth humble you? Are you ready to acknowledge that even your own actions are created by Allah, and that you have no independent power?

Responding to the Objection: How Motion is Known
Ibn Shabib responds to an objection that motion is known only by analogy with human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Shabib responds to the objection that motion is known only by analogy from human actions.

Core Idea
Motion is known through its effects and otherness, not through analogy with human actions.

An objector challenges Ibn Shabib: How can you know motion? You only know it as your own action, and you don't know its modality before it. Why infer motion by analogies? Ibn Shabib's response clarifies that we know motion through the evidence of otherness (ghayriyya) and its effects, not through the modality of human actions. This lesson examines that response and its conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage for the objection (starting from 'وأجاب المعارض له') and the response. Then read the translation.
Notice the shift from the main argument to the dialectical exchange.
2
Identify the objector's point
The objector says: 'How is it? For it is your action, and you did not know the modality of its action before it. So why do you infer motion by analogies?' Understand why this challenges the argument from observation.
The objector claims motion is only known subjectively, not as an objective accident.
3
Analyze the response
The response distinguishes between knowing the modality (kayf) of precedence/posteriority (which is our action) and knowing the otherness (ghayriyya) of motion. We only established evidence for otherness, not that motion is like our actions.
This defends the objectivity of motion as an accident in the body.
4
Connect to the final conclusion
The passage ends by referencing those who denied otherness while affirming precedence. Once the occurrence of motion/rest is established, and the body does not precede them, the body's temporality is proven.
This reinforces the main argument.

And Muhammad ibn Shabib argued for the temporality of bodies by (saying): A body is never free from rest, which is staying, and motion, which is departure. Both are temporal due to what differs regarding the two: the place by what preceded one of them. It is established that one of them has occurred. And if that is called a material in one of the two bodies, then the statement about it is whichever occurred. And in the occurrence of other than what we said, it is known by the occurrence of departure and existence of the body in a place you do not see in the first. And by this necessity which manifested in the body transition, we know motion which is not sensed; for we found difference of state in the sensible and we knew by the thing's resting in the first place and its transition to the second place that what is called its resting in the first state becomes motion and transition in the second state. Refutation of Mu'tazila on Upholding and Response to Objection
Al-Maturidi refutes Mu'tazila on upholding without an upholder and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Maturidi's refutation of the Mu'tazilite concept of 'ibqa bi-la baqa' using the interchangeability of rest and motion, and his response to the objection about bodies never being free of accidents.

Core Idea
Rest and motion can substitute—invalidating upholding without an upholder.

Al-Maturidi begins by dismissing an elaboration, then directly refutes the Mu'tazila who claim that an object can persist without a persisting attribute. He uses the example of rest and motion, showing that each can replace the other, meaning they are distinct attributes. He then answers an objection: if bodies are never free of these accidents, then non-existence requires existence, proving dependence on a creator.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite claim
Read the first part: the Mu'tazila say 'ibqa bi-la baqa' – upholding without an upholder. Al-Maturidi says this is invalidated by the interchangeability of rest and motion.
2
Identify the proof from substitution
Note that rest and motion can each be a substitute for the other, proving they are distinct (ghayriyya). This undermines the Mu'tazilite claim.
This is the key logical move.
3
Analyze the objection and response
The objector says bodies are never free of these accidents. Al-Maturidi responds: each condition of non-existence requires an existence other than itself, otherwise existence would be nullified.
This shows dependence on a cause.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how these two points together refute the Mu'tazilite view and point towards a sustaining Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أطنب فى هذا النوع فتركته لما لا منفعة فيه ، وفيما قال من دليل غيرية السكون والحركة من جواز كون كل واحد منهما بدلا عن الآخر ، وأنهما غيران ما يبطل قول كثير من المعتزلة فى قولهم بالإبقاء بلا بقاء ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم أجاب من عارضه بما كذلك كانت الأجسام غير خالية عما ذكرت أبدا ، فزعم أنه لا يجوز ؛ لما لا يثبت لكل شرط العدم إلا بوجود غير هو في ذلك ، وفى ذلك / بطلان الوجود . واستدل بما سبق ذكره من دخول الدار ، مع [٧] فى طير من يطير -- أن بينها ذراع من جهة واحدة طيرانا مستويا ، لم يحتمل أن يكونا كذلك من غير نهاية الأوليتها ، إذ ارتفاع النهاية يوجب الاجتماع بالاستواء وقد وجد التفاضل . واحتج بما إذ ثبت تضاد الأشياء من الثقل والخفة والحرارة والبرودة ونحو ذلك ، وقد ثبت فساد الشيء من الشيء إلى ما لا أول له ، ثم كان من طبع المتضاد التنافر ، وفى ذلك التباعد وبخاصة إذ جعل أصحاب هذا القول اثنين متباينين فامتزجا وكانا متضادين لم يجز لها الاجتماع بما ذكر ، مع ما كان اختلافها طباعا ، ولو جاز خروجها عن الطباع الذي ذكرت الجاز أن يسخن المبرد ويبرد المسخن ، ولو جاز ذلك لجاز فناؤهما ليخرجا من طبع البقاء وإذا بطل ذا ثبت قول أهل التوحيد في مدبر عليم ألف بين ذلك ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then he elaborated on this type but I have left it because there is no benefit in it. And in what he said as proof of the otherness of rest and motion from the permissibility of each one being a substitute for the other, and that they are two other things, that invalidates the saying of many Mu'tazila in their saying of upholding (ibqa) without an upholder (baqa), and there is no power except with Allah. Then he answered one who objected to him: likewise bodies never are empty of what you mentioned, so he claimed it is not permissible; because each condition of non-existence is only established by an existence other than that, and in that is the nullification of existence. And he argued by what was previously mentioned of entering the house, with [٧] in a bird that flies -- that between them is a cubit from one side in a level flight; it is not possible that they be so without an end to their firstness, because the removal of the end necessitates meeting with equality and difference was found. And he argued by what establishes the opposition of things like heaviness and lightness, heat and cold and so on, and it is established the corruption of a thing from a thing without beginning, then it is from the nature of opposites to repel, and in that is distance, especially when the adherents of this saying make two distinct things that mix and are opposites, it is not permissible for them to combine as mentioned, while their differences were by nature. And if their departure from the nature that was mentioned were permissible, then it would be permissible for the cooler to heat and the heater to cool, and if that were permissible, then their annihilation would be permissible so that they leave the nature of permanence. And when this is invalid, the statement of the people of tawhid is established about a wise disposer (mudabbir 'alim) who brought harmony between them, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 738

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on accidents (rest and motion) and moves to a refutation of the Mu'tazilite concept of 'ibqa bi-la baqa' (upholding without an upholder). Al-Maturidi argues that rest and motion can substitute for each other, proving they are distinct attributes, which undermines the Mu'tazilite claim that persistence occurs without a persisting attribute. He then responds to an objection: if bodies are never free of these accidents, then each condition of non-existence requires an existence other than itself—otherwise existence would be nullified. He uses the analogy of entering a house and a bird's flight to show that differences in distance and equality require a beginning, implying finitude and a cause. The main argument then shifts to opposites: heaviness/lightness, heat/cold, etc., are naturally repellent and cannot combine. If two opposite principles (like light and darkness) mixed, they would not be able to coexist unless a wise disposer harmonized them. This proves the necessity of one wise Creator who reconciles opposites. The passage ends with a declaration of tawhid.

Key Takeaway
The interchangeability of rest and motion refutes upholding without an upholder and shows dependence on a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Commit now to a specific daily reminder (e.g., saying 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' upon waking) to ground yourself in your utter dependence on Him.
?
If the Mu'tazila were right, you could persist without Allah's constant will. Do you live as if that were true? Ask yourself: In which areas of your life—your health, wealth, relationships—do you act as if you are the one who keeps them going? Write down one area and consciously remind yourself today that Allah is its upholder.
?
The interchangeability of rest and motion points to your need for a Creator. When you lie down to sleep or rise to action, do you acknowledge that every movement depends on Allah's preservation? Design a short dhikr (e.g., 'Allahumma bi amrika asumu wa bi amrika astayqiz') to say at bedtime and waking, so you never forget this constant need.

Argument from Finitude: The Analogy of the House and Bird
Al-Maturidi argues for finitude using a bird flight analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Maturidi uses the example of a bird's flight to prove that differences in distance require a beginning, implying a finite world and a Creator.

Core Idea
Differences in flight distance prove a beginning—pointing to a Creator.

Al-Maturidi continues his proof for a single Creator by using a spatial analogy. He recalls a previously mentioned example: entering a house and a bird flying a cubit in level flight. The difference in their distances cannot be infinite; there must be a starting point. This shows finitude and necessitates a cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the analogy: entering a house and a bird flying a cubit from one side in level flight.
The [٧] might be an OCR error; ignore it.
2
Identify the logical point
The key: 'it is not possible that they be so without an end to their firstness.' The difference in distance requires a beginning point.
3
Connect to the argument
Al-Maturidi argues that the removal of the end (infinity) would require equality, but we find difference. Therefore, there must be an end, a beginning, implying a Creator who initiated.
This refutes eternalism.

?

Argument from Opposites: Proof of a Wise Disposer
Al-Maturidi uses opposition of elements to prove a wise harmonizer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Maturidi uses the natural repulsion of opposites and their mixing to prove the necessity of a wise disposer (mudabbir 'alim) who brings harmony.

Core Idea
Opposites repel by nature; their coexistence requires a wise harmonizer.

Al-Maturidi presents a final powerful argument: opposites like heaviness/lightness and heat/cold naturally repel each other. If they were to mix without a harmonizer, they would annihilate. Yet we see them coexist. This proves there is a wise disposer who brought about harmony, affirming the people of tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposites mentioned
Read the passage and note the pairs: heaviness/lightness, heat/cold. These are natural opposites (mutadadd).
2
Understand the nature of opposites
Al-Maturidi states that the nature of opposites is repulsion (tanafur). They cannot combine naturally.
This is a key principle.
3
Analyze the hypothetical mixing
If two distinct opposites mix, they would not be able to coexist. If they could depart from their natures, then the cooler could heat and the heater could cool, leading to annihilation.
This is absurd.
4
Conclude the proof
Since opposites do coexist in creation, there must be a wise disposer (mudabbir 'alim) who harmonized them. This establishes tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أطنب فى هذا النوع فتركته لما لا منفعة فيه ، وفيما قال من دليل غيرية السكون والحركة من جواز كون كل واحد منهما بدلا عن الآخر ، وأنهما غيران ما يبطل قول كثير من المعتزلة فى قولهم بالإبقاء بلا بقاء ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم أجاب من عارضه بما كذلك كانت الأجسام غير خالية عما ذكرت أبدا ، فزعم أنه لا يجوز ؛ لما لا يثبت لكل شرط العدم إلا بوجود غير هو في ذلك ، وفى ذلك / بطلان الوجود . واستدل بما سبق ذكره من دخول الدار ، مع [٧] فى طير من يطير -- أن بينها ذراع من جهة واحدة طيرانا مستويا ، لم يحتمل أن يكونا كذلك من غير نهاية الأوليتها ، إذ ارتفاع النهاية يوجب الاجتماع بالاستواء وقد وجد التفاضل . واحتج بما إذ ثبت تضاد الأشياء من الثقل والخفة والحرارة والبرودة ونحو ذلك ، وقد ثبت فساد الشيء من الشيء إلى ما لا أول له ، ثم كان من طبع المتضاد التنافر ، وفى ذلك التباعد وبخاصة إذ جعل أصحاب هذا القول اثنين متباينين فامتزجا وكانا متضادين لم يجز لها الاجتماع بما ذكر ، مع ما كان اختلافها طباعا ، ولو جاز خروجها عن الطباع الذي ذكرت الجاز أن يسخن المبرد ويبرد المسخن ، ولو جاز ذلك لجاز فناؤهما ليخرجا من طبع البقاء وإذا بطل ذا ثبت قول أهل التوحيد في مدبر عليم ألف بين ذلك ، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then he elaborated on this type but I have left it because there is no benefit in it. And in what he said as proof of the otherness of rest and motion from the permissibility of each one being a substitute for the other, and that they are two other things, that invalidates the saying of many Mu'tazila in their saying of upholding (ibqa) without an upholder (baqa), and there is no power except with Allah. Then he answered one who objected to him: likewise bodies never are empty of what you mentioned, so he claimed it is not permissible; because each condition of non-existence is only established by an existence other than that, and in that is the nullification of existence. And he argued by what was previously mentioned of entering the house, with [٧] in a bird that flies -- that between them is a cubit from one side in a level flight; it is not possible that they be so without an end to their firstness, because the removal of the end necessitates meeting with equality and difference was found. And he argued by what establishes the opposition of things like heaviness and lightness, heat and cold and so on, and it is established the corruption of a thing from a thing without beginning, then it is from the nature of opposites to repel, and in that is distance, especially when the adherents of this saying make two distinct things that mix and are opposites, it is not permissible for them to combine as mentioned, while their differences were by nature. And if their departure from the nature that was mentioned were permissible, then it would be permissible for the cooler to heat and the heater to cool, and if that were permissible, then their annihilation would be permissible so that they leave the nature of permanence. And when this is invalid, the statement of the people of tawhid is established about a wise disposer (mudabbir 'alim) who brought harmony between them, and there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 738

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on accidents (rest and motion) and moves to a refutation of the Mu'tazilite concept of 'ibqa bi-la baqa' (upholding without an upholder). Al-Maturidi argues that rest and motion can substitute for each other, proving they are distinct attributes, which undermines the Mu'tazilite claim that persistence occurs without a persisting attribute. He then responds to an objection: if bodies are never free of these accidents, then each condition of non-existence requires an existence other than itself—otherwise existence would be nullified. He uses the analogy of entering a house and a bird's flight to show that differences in distance and equality require a beginning, implying finitude and a cause. The main argument then shifts to opposites: heaviness/lightness, heat/cold, etc., are naturally repellent and cannot combine. If two opposite principles (like light and darkness) mixed, they would not be able to coexist unless a wise disposer harmonized them. This proves the necessity of one wise Creator who reconciles opposites. The passage ends with a declaration of tawhid.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That opposites can combine naturally without a cause.
Correction: Opposites repel by nature; their harmony proves a wise harmonizer.

Checkpoint

C) That matter is infinite
D) That rest and motion are the same

Key Takeaway
The coexistence of opposites in creation proves the existence of a wise disposer who harmonizes them, affirming tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the world around you—hot and cold, life and death, joy and sorrow—do you truly believe that a wise being is holding them together, or do you sometimes slip into thinking it's all random? What would change in your daily life if you fully internalized that every opposite you encounter is held in place by the will of Al-Mudabbir?
?
The argument says opposites naturally repel—they cannot coexist on their own. Think of a conflict in your own life, a ‘friction’ between two opposing desires or duties. Who or what is harmonizing them in your heart? Have you surrendered that harmony to the Wise Disposer, or are you trying to force it yourself?
?
If the cooler could heat and the heater could cool, the world would fall to annihilation. The very stability of your life—your health, your relationships, your faith—depends on God's wisdom preserving the order. When was the last time you thanked Him for holding together what you cannot? And what is one opposite in your soul that you need to put in His hands today?

Refutation of Dualism: Two Gods Cannot Exist
Al-Maturidi refutes dualism of two creators, especially light and darkness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Maturidi's logical refutation of dualism, showing that belief in two eternal creators leads to contradictions in power, knowledge, and lordship.

Core Idea
Two gods cannot rule; one would always overpower or limit the other.

This passage presents a sophisticated argument against dualism, specifically the Manichaean belief in two eternal principles of light and darkness. Al-Maturidi uses pure reason to show that such duality is logically impossible, exposing the flaws in attributing divinity to beings that display incapacity or ignorance. By examining the necessary attributes of lordship, he demonstrates why only one God can truly exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the logical structure: al-Maturidi begins with a general argument about two gods, then applies it to light and darkness.
The passage is dense; read slowly to follow the reasoning.
2
Identify the core logical dilemma
The argument presents a dilemma: if there are two gods, either each can annihilate the other, or only one can. In either case, incapacity or redundancy follows, which is incompatible with divinity. Find this in the text.
This is the foundational logical move.
3
Apply the argument to light and darkness
Al-Maturidi then applies the general argument to the specific dualist claim that light and darkness are two eternal principles. He shows that light's inability to free itself from darkness proves its incapacity, and darkness's inability to act (evil) shows its limitation.
Notice the examples of ignorance and dispersion.
4
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider why this refutation is important for Islamic theology: it upholds tawhid (oneness of God) by showing that any alleged deity that is limited or dependent cannot be truly divine.
This connects to the broader theme of divine unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : نقول وبالله التوفيق إن القول بأكثر من أن كل واحد منهم يملك إفناء غيره أو لا ، أو يملك الواحد خاصة ، فإن كان الأول أو الثانى لحقها عجز ، مع ما فيه من الجهل بتدبير الإهلاك بالحيل ، إن لم يكن بالقوة ، وإن قدر الواحد بطل غيره ؛ لما لا يتركه يعاديه في ملكه وينازعه في ربوبيته وله قدرة تصفية الملك له واحد لا يخلو من. وبعد ، فإن العاجز الجاهل أحق أن يكون عبدا مربوبا دون أن يكون ربا متلكاً ، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وهذا يبطل على من يقول بالظلمة والنور لما جهل النور حيث وقع في وثاق الآخر ، والظلمة حيث منعت عن عملها وهو الشر في الآخر ، ومع ما تفرقت [۱] أجزاؤهما وتشتتت أحوالهما حتى عجز كل واحد منها أن يظهر سلطانه ويستولى على ماله ، جل ربنا وتعالى عن أن يكون هذه صفته. وأيضا أن القول من أصحاب الإثنين قول بنهاية كل واحد منها من جانب وارتفاعها من سائر الجوانب ، فإن كان الارتفاع دليل القدم لزم الحدث في وجه الحد ، وإن لم يكن لزم الحدث في الكل، مع ما إن لم يقدر النور على تخليص جزوه المتناهى عن يد عدوه بالأجزاء التى لا تتناهى ، ولا كانت تلك الأجزاء قدرت على حفظ ذلك الجزء من يديها قبل الوقوع في وثاقها ، أنى يقدر إذا أراد بعدد الوقوع في وثاق الظلمة والتخليص من قيده . وعلى قول من يجعل الحواس كلها للنور دون الظلمة ، والعلم كله ، وكذلك جميع [ما وصف به الظلمة] مما
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: We say, and success is from Allah, that the statement about more than one (god) either each of them has the power to annihilate the other or not, or only one has such power. If the first or second, incapacity applies to them, along with ignorance in managing destruction through tricks, if not by force. And if one is able, the other is nullified; because he does not leave him to oppose him in his dominion and contend with him in his lordship, and he has the power to purify the dominion for him; one is not free from. Moreover, the incapable and ignorant is more deserving to be a servant and subject rather than a lord and possessor, and there is no power except with Allah. This invalidates the position of those who say with darkness and light, because light was ignorant when it fell into the captivity of the other, and darkness prevented from its action which is evil in the other, and despite the dispersion [1] of their parts and the scattering of their states until each of them was unable to manifest its authority and take possession of its property. Exalted is our Lord and high above having such a description. Also, the statement of the proponents of duality is a statement of each of them having an end from one side and its removal from all other sides. If removal is evidence of pre-eternity, then temporality is necessary on the side of the limit; and if not, then temporality is necessary in the whole. Moreover, if light could not free its finite part from the hand of its enemy by the infinite parts, and those parts could not preserve that part from its hands before falling into its captivity, how can it (light) when it wants, after the occurrence of falling into the captivity of darkness, free itself from its bondage? And according to the saying of those who make all senses belong to light without darkness, and all knowledge, and so are all [that which is described by darkness] from what
Source pages p. 739

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of dualism, specifically the belief in two eternal principles (e.g., light and darkness in Manichaeism). Al-Maturidi presents a logical argument: if there are two gods, either each can annihilate the other, or only one can. In either case, the weaker is incapable, and the stronger makes the other superfluous. Incapacity and ignorance are unbecoming of divinity, so a god that is incapable or ignorant would be a servant, not a lord. He then applies this to the duality of light and darkness: light fell into darkness's captivity, showing ignorance; darkness was prevented from its own action, showing incapacity. Their parts are dispersed, so they cannot rule. Further, if each has a finite side and infinite sides, then either finitude indicates createdness or it does not; either way, they are not eternal. Finally, if light's finite part is trapped and cannot be freed by infinite parts, then neither entity is truly omnipotent. The passage thereby demolishes dualist claims and affirms the oneness of God.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think dualism is refuted simply by stating that two gods would conflict, but al-Maturidi provides a detailed logical argument from power and finitude.
Correction: The refutation is not merely a contradiction but a rigorous demonstration that any two alleged eternal beings would necessarily exhibit incapacity or ignorance, contradicting divinity.

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi's logical refutation shows that belief in two eternal creators leads to contradictions in power, knowledge, and lordship, affirming that only one God can truly exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If you truly internalized that only one God possesses absolute power and knowledge, would you still hesitate to entrust Him with your deepest fears and desires? What concrete step will you take today to surrender a specific area of your life where you act as if you are in control?
?
Al-Maturidi argues that an incapable being is more deserving to be a servant than a lord. In what ways have you been acting like a 'lord' over your own life, relying on your own strength rather than acknowledging your complete dependence on Allah? How can you practice humility today?
?
The dualist believes in two opposing forces. Do you ever subconsciously attribute evil to another source besides Allah, diminishing His exclusive sovereignty over all that happens? How does this passage compel you to reorient your understanding of good and evil under Allah's single will?

Exposition of Dahriyya Views and the Analogy of Colors
Maturidi reports the Dahriyya view of four natures and their dye analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the materialist (Dahriyya) worldview, specifically the naturalists' claim of four natures and their analogy of colors to deny creation.

Core Idea
The materialists use an analogy of dyes to argue that mixture occurs without a creator.

Imam Maturidi begins by presenting the views of the Dahriyya, who advocate the eternity of matter. Among them are the naturalists who attribute all change to the mixture of four fundamental natures: heat, cold, moisture, and dryness. To support their denial of a creator, they offer an analogy of colors mixing without a new event. This lesson lays out their argument so we can later see how Maturidi refutes it.

Guided Path
1
Read the exposition of Dahriyya
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation, to understand the core claims of the naturalists.
Pay attention to the four natures and the dye analogy.
2
Identify the four natures
List the four natures as mentioned: heat, cold, moisture, and dryness. Notice how they claim the world differs by mixture.
3
Understand the dye analogy
The Dahriyya compare the natures to dyes like white, red, black, and green. They claim that mixture causes color changes without creating a new color. Think about how they use this to deny a creator.
This analogy is key to their argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقاويل الدهرية وبيان فسادها ويبس قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم تذكر أقاويل الدهرية على ما ذكره ابن شبيـ وغيره ليظهر مذاهبهم فإن ظهورها أحد أدلة فسادها ، بعد أن يعلم اتفاقهم في قدم طينة العالم واختلافهم في قدم الصنعة وحدثها ، وهذا جملة مذاهبهم . أصحاب الطبائع أنهن أربع : حر وبَرْد ونُدُوة ويبس. واختلف العالم باختلاف الامتزاج منها ، واعتدل ما اعتدل منها باستواء المزاج منها ، وعلى ذلك مجرى الشمس والقمر والنجوم ، ولم يزل يجرى بمثل الذي يجرى كما ترى لا أول للأشياء ، وسموا حركاتها أعراضا ، وضربوا الباطلهم هذا مثلاً من نحو الأصباغ كالبياض والحسرة والسواد والخضرة ، إنها عند الامتزاج على قدر الكثرة والقلة والرقة والكثافة تختلف ألوانها لا أن يكون ثمة حادث لون ، وإن كان ربما يخرج على ما لا يعرف أهل هذه الألوان أن ذلك هم خرج ، فمثله ما ذكروا من الطبائع. قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : فمن تأمل هذا القول بما ضربوا له من المثل وجده يثبت قول أهل التوحيد ؛ لأن الأصباغ لأنفسها لا تمتزج ، ثم هي لو امتزجت لأنفسها خرجت على لون مستسمج مما عد ذلك في العقول فساد للأصباغ ، فإذا مزجها حكيم عليم يعلم عواقب ذلك المزاج خرج متقنا مستحسنا ، ثم كان العالم خرج متقنا ثبت أن الذى به كان العالم عليم حكيم يعرف عواقب الأشياء فأخرجها على ذلك ، وفى ذلك فساد أن يكون تلك الطبائع أو الطينة أو ما سموا الأسماء لنفسها [؟] بحيث يكون على ما عليه يخرج ، فثبت أن الذي أنشأها كذلك مدير حكيم ويجب تكوينها لا من شيء . مع ما كانت الألوان كل لون منها لا يوصف بشيء مما ذكروا من الحرارة والبرودة ؛ إذ قد يكون في الأشياء يغلب عليه لون منها وهو حار، وآخر يغلب عليه ذلك وهو [مع] ذلك بارد ، فثبت أنه لم يكن شيء من ذلك الألوان بما ذكروا ولا ما ذكروا بها ، وفى ذلك ايجاب غير الذي قالوا ، وبالله التوفيق .
Sayings of the materialists (Dahriyya) and demonstration of their corruption. And dryness. Abu Mansur said, may God have mercy on him: Then the sayings of the materialists are mentioned according to what Ibn Shabib and others mentioned, to make their doctrines manifest, for their manifestation is one of the proofs of their corruption, after it is known that they agree on the eternity of the clay of the world and differ on the eternity or temporal origin of the making. This is the sum of their doctrines. The followers of the natures (taba'i') claim that they are four: heat, cold, moisture, and dryness. The world differs by the difference of mixture from them, and what is balanced from it becomes balanced by the equanimity of the mixture. Upon that is the course of the sun, moon, and stars. It has not ceased to proceed in the same manner as you see, with no beginning of things. They call their movements accidents. They strike for their falsehood this example from the likes of dyes, such as whiteness, redness, blackness, and greenness: that upon mixture according to the amount of abundance, scarcity, thinness, and thickness, their colors differ, not that there is a newly occurring color, though sometimes it may come out in a way that the people of these colors do not know that it came out that way. Similarly is what they mentioned concerning the natures. Sheikh Abu Mansur said, may God have mercy on him: Whoever reflects on this statement with the example they gave finds that it confirms the statement of the people of tawhid (divine oneness); because dyes by themselves do not mix, and even if they mixed by themselves, they would come out in a feeble color, counted in intellects as corruption of dyes. But if a wise, knowledgeable mixer who knows the outcomes of that mixture mixes them, it comes out perfect and pleasing. Then, since the world came out perfect, it is established that the one by whom the world came to be is knowing, wise, knowing the outcomes of things, and brought it out accordingly. In that is the corruption of the claim that those natures or the clay or whatever names they named by themselves [are such] that it comes out as it does. So it is established that the one who originated them thus is a wise manager, and their formation must be from nothing. Moreover, the colors—each color cannot be described by anything of what they mentioned of heat and cold; for there are things in which a color predominates and it is hot, and another in which that color predominates and it is cold. So it is established that none of those colors are as they mentioned, nor what they mentioned by them. In that is an affirmation other than what they said. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 740

Commentary
This passage begins with a heading introducing the sayings of the Dahriyya and the demonstration of their corruption. Maturidi then presents their view: they agree on the eternity of the world's matter (clay) but differ on whether its formation is eternal or originated. The naturalists (as'hab al-taba'i') say the world is composed of four natures (heat, cold, moisture, dryness) whose mixture determines all differences. They give an analogy of dyes: colors mix without a new color being created, just as the natures mix without a new event. Maturidi turns this analogy against them: dyes cannot mix by themselves; if they did, the result would be corrupt. Only a wise mixer produces a perfect result. Since the world is perfect, it must have a wise creator. He also notes that colors cannot be linked to heat or cold as the naturalists claim, because the same color can occur in hot and cold objects, disproving their association. Thus the materialist analogy actually confirms the existence of a wise Creator.

الدهرية
طبائع
اصباغ
امتزاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Dahriyya deny a creator by claiming that mixture alone accounts for the world's complexity.
Correction: Maturidi will later show that mixture requires a wise mixer, thus proving a creator.

Key Takeaway
The Dahriyya naturalists reduce all change to the mixture of four eternal natures, using a dye analogy to deny the need for a creator.

?

?
The Dahriyya deny a creator but still see patterns. You believe in Allah—yet do your daily actions reflect trust in Him or in mere causality? What would change if you truly saw Him behind every 'mixture'?
?
When something unexpected happens, do you attribute it to chance or to Allah's wisdom? How can you train yourself to see His involvement in all events, even the 'unexpected' ones?

Maturidi's Refutation: The Dye Analogy and the Argument from Design
Maturidi refutes the Dahriyya dye analogy and argues for a wise Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi turns the materialists' own analogy of dyes into a proof for the existence of a wise Creator.

Core Idea
Dyes cannot mix by themselves; a wise mixer is needed, just as the world needs a wise Creator.

Imam Maturidi now takes the very analogy the Dahriyya used against divine creation and shows it actually supports tawhid. He argues that dyes, if left to themselves, would produce a corrupt mixture—only a knowledgeable mixer can produce a perfect result. Since the world is perfectly ordered, it must have a wise Creator. He further refutes their link between colors and the four natures by showing that the same color can be found in both hot and cold objects.

Guided Path
1
Reflect on the dye analogy
Read Maturidi's response carefully. Notice how he starts with the same analogy but reaches an opposite conclusion.
This is a classic argument from design.
2
Understand the counter-argument
Maturidi argues that dyes by themselves cannot mix; they need a wise mixer. Apply this to the world: its perfection implies a wise Creator.
Consider why a self-mixing world would be chaotic.
3
See the additional refutation
Maturidi also points out that colors are not tied to heat or cold, disproving the naturalists' system entirely.
This strengthens the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقاويل الدهرية وبيان فسادها ويبس قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم تذكر أقاويل الدهرية على ما ذكره ابن شبيـ وغيره ليظهر مذاهبهم فإن ظهورها أحد أدلة فسادها ، بعد أن يعلم اتفاقهم في قدم طينة العالم واختلافهم في قدم الصنعة وحدثها ، وهذا جملة مذاهبهم . أصحاب الطبائع أنهن أربع : حر وبَرْد ونُدُوة ويبس. واختلف العالم باختلاف الامتزاج منها ، واعتدل ما اعتدل منها باستواء المزاج منها ، وعلى ذلك مجرى الشمس والقمر والنجوم ، ولم يزل يجرى بمثل الذي يجرى كما ترى لا أول للأشياء ، وسموا حركاتها أعراضا ، وضربوا الباطلهم هذا مثلاً من نحو الأصباغ كالبياض والحسرة والسواد والخضرة ، إنها عند الامتزاج على قدر الكثرة والقلة والرقة والكثافة تختلف ألوانها لا أن يكون ثمة حادث لون ، وإن كان ربما يخرج على ما لا يعرف أهل هذه الألوان أن ذلك هم خرج ، فمثله ما ذكروا من الطبائع. قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : فمن تأمل هذا القول بما ضربوا له من المثل وجده يثبت قول أهل التوحيد ؛ لأن الأصباغ لأنفسها لا تمتزج ، ثم هي لو امتزجت لأنفسها خرجت على لون مستسمج مما عد ذلك في العقول فساد للأصباغ ، فإذا مزجها حكيم عليم يعلم عواقب ذلك المزاج خرج متقنا مستحسنا ، ثم كان العالم خرج متقنا ثبت أن الذى به كان العالم عليم حكيم يعرف عواقب الأشياء فأخرجها على ذلك ، وفى ذلك فساد أن يكون تلك الطبائع أو الطينة أو ما سموا الأسماء لنفسها [؟] بحيث يكون على ما عليه يخرج ، فثبت أن الذي أنشأها كذلك مدير حكيم ويجب تكوينها لا من شيء . مع ما كانت الألوان كل لون منها لا يوصف بشيء مما ذكروا من الحرارة والبرودة ؛ إذ قد يكون في الأشياء يغلب عليه لون منها وهو حار، وآخر يغلب عليه ذلك وهو [مع] ذلك بارد ، فثبت أنه لم يكن شيء من ذلك الألوان بما ذكروا ولا ما ذكروا بها ، وفى ذلك ايجاب غير الذي قالوا ، وبالله التوفيق .
Sayings of the materialists (Dahriyya) and demonstration of their corruption. And dryness. Abu Mansur said, may God have mercy on him: Then the sayings of the materialists are mentioned according to what Ibn Shabib and others mentioned, to make their doctrines manifest, for their manifestation is one of the proofs of their corruption, after it is known that they agree on the eternity of the clay of the world and differ on the eternity or temporal origin of the making. This is the sum of their doctrines. The followers of the natures (taba'i') claim that they are four: heat, cold, moisture, and dryness. The world differs by the difference of mixture from them, and what is balanced from it becomes balanced by the equanimity of the mixture. Upon that is the course of the sun, moon, and stars. It has not ceased to proceed in the same manner as you see, with no beginning of things. They call their movements accidents. They strike for their falsehood this example from the likes of dyes, such as whiteness, redness, blackness, and greenness: that upon mixture according to the amount of abundance, scarcity, thinness, and thickness, their colors differ, not that there is a newly occurring color, though sometimes it may come out in a way that the people of these colors do not know that it came out that way. Similarly is what they mentioned concerning the natures. Sheikh Abu Mansur said, may God have mercy on him: Whoever reflects on this statement with the example they gave finds that it confirms the statement of the people of tawhid (divine oneness); because dyes by themselves do not mix, and even if they mixed by themselves, they would come out in a feeble color, counted in intellects as corruption of dyes. But if a wise, knowledgeable mixer who knows the outcomes of that mixture mixes them, it comes out perfect and pleasing. Then, since the world came out perfect, it is established that the one by whom the world came to be is knowing, wise, knowing the outcomes of things, and brought it out accordingly. In that is the corruption of the claim that those natures or the clay or whatever names they named by themselves [are such] that it comes out as it does. So it is established that the one who originated them thus is a wise manager, and their formation must be from nothing. Moreover, the colors—each color cannot be described by anything of what they mentioned of heat and cold; for there are things in which a color predominates and it is hot, and another in which that color predominates and it is cold. So it is established that none of those colors are as they mentioned, nor what they mentioned by them. In that is an affirmation other than what they said. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 740

Commentary
This passage begins with a heading introducing the sayings of the Dahriyya and the demonstration of their corruption. Maturidi then presents their view: they agree on the eternity of the world's matter (clay) but differ on whether its formation is eternal or originated. The naturalists (as'hab al-taba'i') say the world is composed of four natures (heat, cold, moisture, dryness) whose mixture determines all differences. They give an analogy of dyes: colors mix without a new color being created, just as the natures mix without a new event. Maturidi turns this analogy against them: dyes cannot mix by themselves; if they did, the result would be corrupt. Only a wise mixer produces a perfect result. Since the world is perfect, it must have a wise creator. He also notes that colors cannot be linked to heat or cold as the naturalists claim, because the same color can occur in hot and cold objects, disproving their association. Thus the materialist analogy actually confirms the existence of a wise Creator.

Key Terms
التوحيد
حكيم
امتزاج
طبائع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dye analogy supports materialism by showing mixture without creation.
Correction: Maturidi shows the analogy actually proves the need for a wise creator.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about dyes by themselves?
A) Dyes mix perfectly.
B) Dyes cannot mix and would produce a corrupt color.
C) Dyes are eternal.
D) Dyes are the four natures.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's refutation of the Dahriyya dye analogy demonstrates that the world's perfection necessitates a wise Creator, and their own example is turned against them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the perfect order of the world—from the movement of galaxies to the complexity of a single cell—have you truly internalized that this demands a wise Creator, or do you still mentally attribute it to 'nature' or 'chance'? What would it take for that acknowledgment to move from your mind to your heart and change how you live today?
?
The Dahriyya used an analogy to deny God, but Maturidi showed that it actually proves His existence. Are there arguments or assumptions in your own life that you use to distance yourself from God's reality? Will you honestly examine them and see if they too point back to Him?
?
If the world is perfectly made by a wise Creator, then your life—with all its events—is also part of that wise plan. Are you living as if you truly believe that every situation you face is perfectly designed for you by the All-Wise, or do you resist and complain? What one specific area of your life can you surrender to His wisdom starting now?

Refutation from the Diversity of Tastes
Maturidi presents an argument from taste diversity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the diversity of tastes in a single substance points to a wise arranger and refutes materialism.

Core Idea
A single nature cannot produce multiple tastes without a controller.

Maturidi continues his refutation of the materialists by pointing to the observable diversity of tastes in nature. He argues that a single substance — with one color and one nature — can produce different tastes like saltiness, sourness, etc., or even be tasteless. This variation cannot arise from the substance itself, indicating an external arrangement by a powerful, knowing being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the observation about taste diversity.
2
Identify the phenomenon
Maturidi describes that a single substance with one color and nature can yield different tastes. What is the key observation?
The tastes include saltiness, sourness, bitterness, or no taste at all.
3
Understand the inference
The diversity cannot be caused by the substance itself. It must be by the arrangement of someone who can make each as He wills, without physical causes.
4
Note the emphasis on 'without causes'
Maturidi stresses that this arrangement is without causes (min ghayri asbab), refuting materialist natural causation.
This undermines the materialist reliance on inherent properties.
5
Appreciate the conclusion
The argument leads to the necessity of a divine controller who arranges tastes in substances.
This is a direct refutation of atheistic materialism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك يجد ما فيها من الطعوم مختلفة حتى يكون بلون واحد وطبيعة واحده تخرج على نوع من الطعم نحو الملوحة أو الحموضة أو المرارة أو الطينة التي لا يضرب إلى شيء من ذلك ، ثبت أن ذلك كان بتدبير من يملك جعل كل على ما شاء من غير أسباب ، ولا قوة إلا بالله على أن هذه الطبائع لا يخلو من أن يكون جواهر أو أعراضا، فإن كانت جواهر صيرت بالأعراض التي اعترضت فيها على ما ذكر من الاختلاف الاجتماع والافتراق ، ولولاهما لكان كل جوهر من ذلك متفرقا ، ودل اختلاف الجواهر مع اجتماع الأخلاط فيها على غلبة الأعراض عليها ، وأنها تصرفها من حال إلى حال ، ثم كانت الأعراض لأنفسها لا تقوم ولا تقدح في الأشياء ، ثبت أنها عملت فيها هذا العمل يمن يعلم أنها تعمل كذا ، ولم يجز أن يكون يعلم أحد ذلك إلا بمن يملك جعل تلك الجواهر يصلح لاحتمال تلك الأعراض ، ومحال علم مثله إلا بمن يجعلها كذلك ، وفى ذلك لزوم القول بواحد عليم قادر لا يخفى عليه شيء ولا يصعب عليه تكوين ما يريد كونه . وإن كانت أعراضا فمحال وجودها
And similarly one finds what is in it of diverse tastes, so that a single color and single nature yields a type of taste, like saltiness, sourness, bitterness, or the clay that does not tend toward any of that. It is established that that was by the arrangement of one who has the power to make each as He wills without causes. And there is no power except with God. Moreover, these natures are not free from being either substances or accidents. If they are substances, they are made by the accidents that befall them, according to what was mentioned of difference, combination, and separation. Without these, every substance would be separate. And the difference of substances together with the mixing of humors in them indicates the dominance of accidents over them, and that they turn them from state to state. Then, since accidents by themselves do not subsist and do not affect things, it is established that they performed this action by one who knows that they act so. And it is not possible that anyone knows that except one who has the power to make those substances fit to receive those accidents. Knowledge of the like is impossible except by one who makes them so. In that is the necessity of affirming one who is knowing, powerful, nothing is hidden from Him, and nothing is difficult for Him to bring into existence what He wills. But if they are accidents, their existence is impossible.
Source pages p. 741

Commentary
This passage presents two additional arguments against the materialists. First, from the diversity of tastes: a single substance with one color and nature can produce different tastes (salty, sour, bitter) or no taste at all. This cannot happen by itself but requires a wise arranger who makes each thing as He wills without physical causes. Second, the author argues about the natures themselves: they must be either substances or accidents. If they are substances, they undergo change through accidents (combination, separation). Since accidents cannot exist by themselves, the change must be caused by an external agent who knows and has power to make the substances fit to receive accidents. This leads to affirming a single, knowing, powerful God. If they are accidents, they cannot subsist at all, refuting the materialist claim.

Key Terms
طعوم
تدبير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the diversity of tastes in a single substance indicate?
A) The substance's own nature
B) Random chance
C) An external arranger
D) The substance's color

Key Takeaway
The diversity of tastes in a single substance proves that the universe is governed by a wise, powerful being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you eat a piece of fruit and taste its sweetness, does it ever occur to you that this taste is not a property of the fruit itself but a direct gift from Allah? What would change in your gratitude if you remembered this at every meal?
?
This lesson argues that diversity in simple things like taste points to a Controller. Are there areas of your life where you attribute outcomes to 'natural causes' or 'luck' instead of seeing Allah's direct hand? Confess one such area and commit to seeing Him in it today.
?
Maturidi says 'without causes' — do you have a habit of explaining away miracles or blessings as mere coincidence? How would your reliance on Allah increase if you truly believed He is the direct cause of every variation in your life?

Refutation from the Substance/Accident Distinction
Maturidi argues from substance/accident distinction to necessary existence of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the dependence of substances on accidents leads to affirming a single, knowing, powerful God.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot subsist by themselves; their control over substances proves an external agent.

Maturidi deepens his argument by analyzing the nature of material things. He posits that all natures are either substances or accidents. If they are substances, they undergo change through accidents like combination and separation. Since accidents cannot exist independently, the changes they cause must be directed by a knowing, powerful being. This argument establishes the necessity of a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Note the conditional structure 'if substances... then...' and 'if accidents... then...'.
2
Understand the conditional: if substances
Maturidi says if natures are substances, they are affected by accidents like combination and separation. The mixing of humors shows accidents dominate substances.
Accidents turn substances from state to state.
3
Analyze the role of accidents
Accidents by themselves do not subsist and do not affect things. Therefore, their action on substances must be by an external agent who knows they act and makes substances fit to receive accidents.
4
Draw the conclusion
This leads to affirming one who is knowing, powerful, with no hidden or difficult thing. That is God.
5
Consider the other branch: if accidents
If natures were accidents, their existence would be impossible (since accidents depend on substances). This further refutes materialism.
The text ends mid-sentence but the argument is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And similarly one finds what is in it of diverse tastes, so that a single color and single nature yields a type of taste, like saltiness, sourness, bitterness, or the clay that does not tend toward any of that. It is established that that was by the arrangement of one who has the power to make each as He wills without causes. And there is no power except with God. If they are substances, they are made by the accidents that befall them, according to what was mentioned of difference, combination, and separation. Without these, every substance would be separate. And the difference of substances together with the mixing of humors in them indicates the dominance of accidents over them, and that they turn them from state to state. Then, since accidents by themselves do not subsist and do not affect things, it is established that they performed this action by one who knows that they act so. And it is not possible that anyone knows that except one who has the power to make those substances fit to receive those accidents. Knowledge of the like is impossible except by one who makes them so. In that is the necessity of affirming one who is knowing, powerful, nothing is hidden from Him, and nothing is difficult for Him to bring into existence what He wills. But if they are accidents, their existence is impossible.

This passage presents two additional arguments against the materialists. First, from the diversity of tastes: a single substance with one color and nature can produce different tastes (salty, sour, bitter) or no taste at all. This cannot happen by itself but requires a wise arranger who makes each thing as He wills without physical causes. ?

Will you consciously attribute the next change you experience to Him alone?
?
If accidents cannot even subsist without a substance, how much more do your own fleeting thoughts and decisions depend on the One who made you receptive to them? Examine your heart: do you live as if your choices are independent? What would change in your daily routine if you genuinely accepted that every thought you have is placed in you by a knowing, powerful God?
?
The materialist claims natures are self-sufficient, but your own experience of constant change—from youth to old age, health to sickness—testifies that you are not self-subsistent. How does this truth confront your sense of autonomy? Write down one specific action you will take today to humble yourself before the One who controls your every state.

The Dye Analogy and the Argument from Colors
Maturidi presents the dye analogy and the argument from colors to refute self-acting natures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that if natures acted by themselves, they would produce disorder, but the observed order in colors points to a wise creator.

Core Idea
Self-acting natures cannot produce order; a wise chemist is needed.

Maturidi uses a simple analogy: when dyes mix by themselves, the result is ugly. But when mixed by a wise chemist, the result is beautiful. He then applies this to the world: its perfection proves a wise creator, not blind natures.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy and its application
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice how Maturidi starts with a concrete example of dyes and then applies it to the entire world.
The analogy is the core of this argument.
2
Observe the point about colors and temperature
Maturidi adds that colors do not correlate with heat and cold, disproving that natures (with inherent properties) cause colors. This strengthens his refutation.
This observation shows that colors are independent of supposed natures.
3
Understand the refutation of natures
By showing that natures cannot produce consistent order, Maturidi concludes that a wise manager must be responsible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
